	<div class="vector-menu-content">
		
		<ul class="vector-menu-content-list">

				<div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body" aria-labelledby="firstHeading" data-mw-ve-target-container>
					<div class="vector-body-before-content">
							<div class="mw-indicators">
		</div>

						<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
					</div>
					<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-content-subtitle"></div></div>
					
					
					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Calendar used by several pre-Columbian Mesoamerican cultures</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>
<figure class="mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:East_side_of_stela_C,_Quirigua.PNG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/East_side_of_stela_C%2C_Quirigua.PNG/200px-East_side_of_stela_C%2C_Quirigua.PNG" decoding="async" width="200" height="461" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7a/East_side_of_stela_C%2C_Quirigua.PNG 1.5x" data-file-width="251" data-file-height="579" /></a><figcaption>East side of stela C, Quirigua with the mythical creation date of 13 <a href="/wiki/Baktun" title="Baktun">baktuns</a>, 0 katuns, 0 tuns, 0 winals, 0 kins, 4 Ahau 8 Cumku – August 11, 3114&#160;BCE in the <a href="/wiki/Proleptic_Gregorian_calendar" title="Proleptic Gregorian calendar">proleptic Gregorian calendar</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The <b>Mesoamerican Long Count calendar</b> is a non-repeating <a href="/wiki/Vigesimal" title="Vigesimal">base-20</a> and base-18 calendar used by <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian" class="mw-redirect" title="Pre-Columbian">pre-Columbian</a> <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerican</a> cultures, most notably the <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">Maya</a>. For this reason, it is often known as the <b>Maya</b> <b>Long Count calendar</b>. Using a modified vigesimal tally, the Long Count calendar identifies a day by counting the number of days passed since a <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_creation_myths" title="Mesoamerican creation myths">mythical creation date</a> that corresponds to August 11, 3114 <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">BCE</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Proleptic_Gregorian_calendar" title="Proleptic Gregorian calendar">proleptic Gregorian calendar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>a<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Long Count calendar was widely used on monuments.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Background">Background</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerican_Long_Count_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The two most widely used calendars in pre-Columbian Mesoamerica were the 260-day <a href="/wiki/Tzolk%CA%BCin" title="Tzolkʼin">Tzolkʼin</a> and the 365-day <a href="/wiki/Haab%CA%BC" title="Haabʼ">Haabʼ</a>. The equivalent Aztec calendars are known in <a href="/wiki/Nahuatl" title="Nahuatl">Nahuatl</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Tonalpohualli" class="mw-redirect" title="Tonalpohualli">Tonalpohualli</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xiuhpohualli" class="mw-redirect" title="Xiuhpohualli">Xiuhpohualli</a>, respectively.
</p><p>The combination of a Haabʼ and a Tzolkʼin date identifies a day in a combination which does not occur again for 18,980&#160;days (52&#160;Haabʼ cycles of 365&#160;days equals 73&#160;Tzolkʼin cycles of 260&#160;days, approximately 52&#160;years), a period known as the <a href="/wiki/Calendar_Round" class="mw-redirect" title="Calendar Round">Calendar Round</a>. To identify days over periods longer than this, Mesoamericans used the Long Count calendar.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Long_Count_periods">Long Count periods</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerican_Long_Count_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Long Count periods"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1251242444">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+link+.ambox{margin-top:-1px}html body.mediawiki .mw-parser-output .ambox.mbox-small-left{margin:4px 1em 4px 0;overflow:hidden;width:238px;border-collapse:collapse;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em}.mw-parser-output .ambox-speedy{border-left:10px solid #b32424;background-color:#fee7e6}.mw-parser-output .ambox-delete{border-left:10px solid #b32424}.mw-parser-output .ambox-content{border-left:10px solid #f28500}.mw-parser-output .ambox-style{border-left:10px solid #fc3}.mw-parser-output .ambox-move{border-left:10px solid #9932cc}.mw-parser-output .ambox-protection{border-left:10px solid #a2a9b1}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-text{border:none;padding:0.25em 0;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-empty-cell{border:none;padding:0;width:1px}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-image-div{width:52px}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .ambox{display:none!important}}</style><table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Mesoamerican_Long_Count_calendar" title="Special:EditPage/Mesoamerican Long Count calendar">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>&#32;in this section. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">August 2017</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:La_Mojarra_Estela_1_(Escritura_superior).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/La_Mojarra_Estela_1_%28Escritura_superior%29.jpg/199px-La_Mojarra_Estela_1_%28Escritura_superior%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="199" height="266" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/La_Mojarra_Estela_1_%28Escritura_superior%29.jpg/299px-La_Mojarra_Estela_1_%28Escritura_superior%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/La_Mojarra_Estela_1_%28Escritura_superior%29.jpg/399px-La_Mojarra_Estela_1_%28Escritura_superior%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="3456" /></a><figcaption>Detail showing columns of glyphs from a portion of the 2nd century&#160;CE <a href="/wiki/La_Mojarra_Stela_1" title="La Mojarra Stela 1">La Mojarra Stela 1</a>. The left column gives a Long Count date of 8.5.16.9.7, or 156&#160;CE. The other columns visible are glyphs from the <a href="/wiki/Epi-Olmec_script" class="mw-redirect" title="Epi-Olmec script">Epi-Olmec script</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The Long Count calendar identifies a date by counting the number of days from a starting date that is generally calculated to be August 11, 3114&#160;BCE in the proleptic Gregorian calendar or September 6 in the Julian calendar (or −3113 in astronomical year numbering). There has been much debate over the precise correlation between the Western calendars and the Long Count calendars. The August 11 date is based on the GMT correlation.
</p><p>The completion of 13 <a href="/wiki/Baktun" title="Baktun">bʼakʼtuns</a> (August 11, 3114&#160;BCE) marks the Creation of the world of human beings according to the Maya. On this day, Raised-up-Sky-Lord caused three stones to be set by associated gods at Lying-Down-Sky, First-Three-Stone-Place. Because the sky still lay on the primordial sea, it was black. The setting of the three stones centered the cosmos which allowed the sky to be raised, revealing the <span class="nowrap">sun.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</p><p>Rather than using a base&#160;10 scheme, the Long Count days were tallied in a modified base-20 scheme. In a pure base&#160;20 scheme, 0.0.0.1.5 is equal to 25 and 0.0.0.2.0 is equal to 40. The Long Count is not pure base-20, however, since the second digit from the right (and only that digit) rolls over to zero when it reaches 18.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Thus 0.0.1.0.0 does not represent 400&#160;days, but rather only 360 days and 0.0.0.17.19 represents 359 days.
</p><p>The name <i>bʼakʼtun</i> was invented by modern scholars. The numbered Long Count was no longer in use by the time the Spanish arrived in the <a href="/wiki/Yucat%C3%A1n_Peninsula" title="Yucatán Peninsula">Yucatán Peninsula</a>, although unnumbered <a href="/wiki/K%CA%BCatun" title="Kʼatun">kʼatuns</a> and tuns were still in use. Instead the Maya were using an abbreviated <a href="/wiki/Maya_calendar#Short_count" title="Maya calendar">Short Count</a>.
</p>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Mesoamerican_numerals">Mesoamerican numerals</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerican_Long_Count_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Mesoamerican numerals"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Maya.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Maya.svg/220px-Maya.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="282" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Maya.svg/330px-Maya.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Maya.svg/440px-Maya.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="250" data-file-height="320" /></a><figcaption>Maya numerals</figcaption></figure>
<p>Long Count dates are written with Mesoamerican numerals, as shown on this table. A dot represents <i>1</i> while a bar equals <i>5</i>. The shell glyph was used to represent the zero concept. The Long Count calendar required the use of zero as a place-holder and presents one of the earliest uses of the <a href="/wiki/0_(number)#History" class="mw-redirect" title="0 (number)">zero concept in history</a>.
</p><p>On Maya monuments, the Long Count syntax is more complex. The date sequence is given once, at the beginning of the inscription and opens with the so-called ISIG (Introductory Series Initial Glyph) which reads <i>tzik-a(h) habʼ [patron of Haabʼ month]</i> ("revered was the year-count with the patron [of the month]").<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  Next come the 5 digits of the Long Count, followed by the Calendar Round (tzolkʼin and Haabʼ) and <a href="/wiki/Maya_calendar#Supplementary_Series" title="Maya calendar">supplementary series</a>. The supplementary series is optional and contains lunar data, for example, the age of the moon on the day and the calculated length of current <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/lunation" class="extiw" title="wikt:lunation">lunation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>b<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The text then continues with whatever activity occurred on that date.
</p><p>A drawing of a full Maya Long Count inscription is shown <a href="#Calculating_a_full_Long_Count_date">below</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Earliest_Long_Counts">Earliest Long Counts</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerican_Long_Count_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Earliest Long Counts"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The earliest contemporaneous Long Count inscription yet discovered is on Stela&#160;2 at <a href="/wiki/Chiapa_de_Corzo_(Mesoamerican_site)" title="Chiapa de Corzo (Mesoamerican site)">Chiapa de Corzo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chiapas" title="Chiapas">Chiapas</a>, Mexico, showing a date of 36&#160;BCE, although Stela 2 from <a href="/wiki/Takalik_Abaj" title="Takalik Abaj">Takalik Abaj</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guatemala" title="Guatemala">Guatemala</a> might be earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a class="external autonumber" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:Abaj_Takalik_Stela2.jpg">[2]</a> Takalik Abaj Stela 2's highly battered Long Count inscription shows 7&#160;<i>bak'tuns</i>, followed by <i>k'atuns</i> with a tentative 6&#160;coefficient, but that could also be 11 or 16, giving the range of possible dates to fall between 236 and 19&#160;BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>c<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Some sources identify just Baktun 7, though Katun 6–16 is at least consistent with Mora-Marín (2005)&#39;s 236–19&#160;BCE (February 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Although Takalik Abaj Stela&#160;2 remains controversial, this table includes it, as well as six other artifacts with the eight oldest Long Count inscriptions according to Dartmouth professor Vincent H. Malmström (two of the artifacts contain two dates and Malmström does not include Takalik Abaj Stela&#160;2).<sup id="cite_ref-malmstrom1997_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-malmstrom1997-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Interpretations of inscriptions on some artifacts differ.<sup id="cite_ref-malmstrom1997_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-malmstrom1997-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-marcus1976_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-marcus1976-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-riese1988_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-riese1988-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<p>Of the six sites, three are on the western edge of the Maya homeland and three are several hundred kilometers further west, leading some researchers to believe that the Long Count calendar predates the Maya.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> La Mojarra Stela&#160;1, the Tuxtla Statuette, Tres Zapotes Stela&#160;C and Chiapa Stela&#160;2 are all inscribed in an <a href="/wiki/Epi-Olmec_script" class="mw-redirect" title="Epi-Olmec script">Epi-Olmec</a>, not Maya, style.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> El Baúl Stela&#160;2, on the other hand, was created in the <a href="/wiki/Izapa" title="Izapa">Izapan</a> style.
</p><p>The first unequivocally Maya artifact is Stela&#160;29 from <a href="/wiki/Tikal" title="Tikal">Tikal</a>, with the Long Count date of 292&#160;CE (8.12.14.8.15), more than 300&#160;years after Stela&#160;2 from Chiapa de Corzo.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>More recently, with the discovery in Guatemala of the <a href="/wiki/San_Bartolo_(Maya_site)" title="San Bartolo (Maya site)">San Bartolo (Maya site)</a> stone block text (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 300&#160;BCE),<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> it has been argued that this text celebrates an upcoming time period ending celebration. This time period may have been projected to end sometime between 7.3.0.0.0 (295&#160;BCE) and 7.5.0.0.0 (256&#160;BCE).<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  Besides being the earliest Maya hieroglyphic text so far uncovered, this would arguably be the earliest evidence to date of Long Count notation in Mesoamerica.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Correlations_between_Western_calendars_and_the_Long_Count">Correlations between Western calendars and the Long Count</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerican_Long_Count_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Correlations between Western calendars and the Long Count"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Estela_C_de_Tres_Zapotes.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Estela_C_de_Tres_Zapotes.jpg/250px-Estela_C_de_Tres_Zapotes.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="329" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/59/Estela_C_de_Tres_Zapotes.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="363" data-file-height="477" /></a><figcaption>The back of Stela&#160;C from <a href="/wiki/Tres_Zapotes" title="Tres Zapotes">Tres Zapotes</a>, an Olmec archaeological site.<br />This is the second oldest Long Count date yet discovered. The numerals 7.16.6.16.18 translate to September 1, 32&#160;BCE (Gregorian). The glyphs surrounding the date are what is thought to be one of the few surviving examples of <a href="/wiki/Epi-Olmec_script" class="mw-redirect" title="Epi-Olmec script">Epi-Olmec script</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The Maya and Western calendars are correlated by using a <a href="/wiki/Julian_day_number" class="mw-redirect" title="Julian day number">Julian day number</a> (JDN) of the starting date of the current creation — 13.0.0.0.0, 4 <a href="/wiki/Ajaw" title="Ajaw">Ajaw</a>, 8 Kumkʼu.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>d<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This is referred to as a "correlation constant". The generally accepted correlation constant is the Modified Thompson&#160;2, "<a href="/wiki/Joseph_T._Goodman" title="Joseph T. Goodman">Goodman</a>–Martinez–<a href="/wiki/J._Eric_S._Thompson" title="J. Eric S. Thompson">Thompson</a>", or GMT correlation of 584,283&#160;days. Using the GMT correlation, the current creation started on September 6, −3113 (<a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian</a> astronomical) – August 11, 3114&#160;BCE in the <a href="/wiki/Proleptic_Gregorian_calendar" title="Proleptic Gregorian calendar">Proleptic Gregorian calendar</a>. The study of correlating the Maya and western calendar is referred to as the correlation question.<sup id="cite_ref-mesoweb_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mesoweb-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-google_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-alignment2012_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alignment2012-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hermetic_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hermetic-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-famsi_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-famsi-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The GMT correlation is also called the <b>11.16</b> correlation.
</p><p>In <i>Breaking the Maya Code</i>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_D._Coe" title="Michael D. Coe">Michael D. Coe</a> writes: "In spite of oceans of ink that have been spilled on the subject, there now is not the slightest chance that these three scholars (conflated to G-M-T when talking about the correlation) were not right&#160;...".<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The evidence for the GMT correlation is historical, astronomical and archaeological:
</p><p><b>Historical</b>: Calendar Round dates with a corresponding <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian date</a> are recorded in <a href="/wiki/Diego_de_Landa" title="Diego de Landa">Diego de Landa</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Relaci%C3%B3n_de_las_cosas_de_Yucat%C3%A1n" title="Relación de las cosas de Yucatán">Relación de las cosas de Yucatán</a></i> (written circa 1566), the Chronicle of Oxcutzkab and the books of <a href="/wiki/Chilam_Balam" title="Chilam Balam">Chilam Balam</a>. De Landa records a date that is a Tun ending in the <a href="/wiki/Maya_calendar#Short_Count" title="Maya calendar">Short Count</a>. Oxkutzcab contains 12&#160;Tun endings. Bricker and Bricker find that only the GMT correlation is consistent with these dates.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Book of Chilam Balam of Chumayel<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> contains the only colonial reference to classic long-count dates. The Julian calendar date of 11.16.0.0.0 (November 2, 1539) confirms the GMT correlation.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Annals_of_the_Cakchiquels" title="Annals of the Cakchiquels">Annals of the Cakchiquels</a> contains numerous Tzolkʼin dates correlated with European dates. These confirm the GMT correlation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERecinos195333_Recinos_gives_2&amp;nbsp;Tihax_(Etzʼnabʼ)_is_September_10,_1541(&#91;&#91;Julian_calendar&#124;Julian&#93;&#93;)._The_date_which_marked_the_destruction_of_the_city_of_Guatemala_founded_at_the_foot_of_the_Volcano_of_Agua._&quot;After_a_period_of_heavy_rain_there_was_an_earthquake_and_then_a_flood_of_water_(a_&#91;&#91;lahar&#93;&#93;)_that_destroyed_the_old_city_of_Guatemala&quot;_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERecinos195333_Recinos_gives_2&amp;nbsp;Tihax_(Etzʼnabʼ)_is_September_10,_1541([[Julian_calendar|Julian]])._The_date_which_marked_the_destruction_of_the_city_of_Guatemala_founded_at_the_foot_of_the_Volcano_of_Agua._&quot;After_a_period_of_heavy_rain_there_was_an_earthquake_and_then_a_flood_of_water_(a_[[lahar]])_that_destroyed_the_old_city_of_Guatemala&quot;-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Weeks, Sachse and Prager transcribed three divinatory calendars from highland Guatemala. They found that the 1772 calendar confirms the GMT correlation.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The fall of the capital city of the Aztec Empire, <a href="/wiki/Tenochtitlan" title="Tenochtitlan">Tenochtitlan</a>, occurred on August 13, 1521.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A number of different chroniclers wrote that the <a href="/wiki/Tzolk%CA%BCin" title="Tzolkʼin">Tzolkʼin</a> (<a href="/wiki/Tonalpohualli" class="mw-redirect" title="Tonalpohualli">Tonalpohualli</a>) date of the event was 1 Snake.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Post-conquest scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Bernardino_de_Sahag%C3%BAn" title="Bernardino de Sahagún">Sahagún</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diego_Dur%C3%A1n" title="Diego Durán">Durán</a> recorded <a href="/wiki/Tonalpohualli" class="mw-redirect" title="Tonalpohualli">Tonalpohualli</a> dates with a calendar date. Many indigenous communities in the Mexican states of Veracruz, Oaxaca and Chiapas<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and in Guatemala, principally those speaking the Mayan languages Ixil, Mam, Pokomchí and Quiché, keep the Tzolkʼin and in many cases the Haabʼ.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These are all consistent with the GMT correlation. Munro Edmonsen studied 60 Mesoamerican calendars, 20 of which have known correlations to European calendars, and found remarkable consistency among them and that only the GMT correlation fits the historical, ethnographic and astronomical evidence.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><b>Astronomical</b>: Any correct correlation must match the astronomical content of classic inscriptions. The GMT correlation does an excellent job of matching lunar data in the <a href="/wiki/Maya_calendar#Supplementary_Series" title="Maya calendar">supplementary series</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-astras-stargate_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-astras-stargate-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For example: An inscription at the Temple of the Sun at <a href="/wiki/Palenque" title="Palenque">Palenque</a> records that on Long Count 9.16.4.10.8 there were 26 days completed in a 30-day lunation.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This Long Count is also the entry date for the eclipse table of the <a href="/wiki/Dresden_Codex" title="Dresden Codex">Dresden Codex</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>e<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Using the third method, the Palenque system,<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the new moon would have been the first evening when one could look to the west after sunset and see the thin crescent moon. Given our modern ability to know exactly where to look, when the crescent Moon is favorably located, from an excellent site, on rare occasions, using binoculars or a telescope, observers can see and photograph the crescent moon less than one day after conjunction. Generally, most observers cannot see the new Moon with the naked eye until the first evening when the lunar phase day is at least 1.5.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> If one assumes that the new moon is the first day when the lunar phase day is at least 1.5 at six in the evening in time zone UTC&#8722;6 (the time zone of the Maya area), the GMT correlation will match many lunar inscriptions exactly. In this example the lunar phase day was 27.7 (26&#160;days counting from zero) at 6&#160;pm after a conjunction at 1:25&#160;am <span class="nowrap">October 10, 755</span> and a new Moon when the lunar phase day was 1.7 at 6&#160;pm on <span class="nowrap">October 11, 755</span> (Julian calendar). This works well for many but not all lunar inscriptions.
</p><p>Modern astronomers refer to the conjunction of the Sun and Moon (the time when the Sun and Moon have the same ecliptic longitude) as the new moon. But Mesoamerican astronomy was <i>observational</i>, not theoretical. The people of Mesoamerica did not know about the <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Copernican</a> nature of the solar system — they had no theoretical understanding of the orbital nature of the heavenly bodies. Some authors analyze the lunar inscriptions based on this modern understanding of the motions of the Moon but there is no evidence that the Mesoamericans shared it.
</p><p>The first method seems to have been used for other inscriptions such as Quirgua stela E (9.17.0.0.0). By the third method, that stela should show a moon age of 26&#160;days, but in fact it records a new moon.<sup id="cite_ref-google2_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google2-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Using the GMT correlation at six AM in the time zone UTC&#8722;6, this would be 2.25&#160;days before conjunction, so it could record the first day when one could not see the waning moon.
</p><p>Fuls<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> analysed these inscriptions and found strong evidence for the Palenque system and the GMT correlation; however, he cautioned: "Analysis of the Lunar Series shows that at least two different methods and formulas were used to calculate the moon's age and position in the six-month cycle&#160;..." which gives eclipse seasons when the Moon is near its <a href="/wiki/Ascending_node" class="mw-redirect" title="Ascending node">ascending</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ascending_node" class="mw-redirect" title="Ascending node">descending</a> node and an <a href="/wiki/Eclipse" title="Eclipse">eclipse</a> is likely to occur. Dates converted using the GMT correlation agree closely with the Dresden Codex eclipse tables.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Dresden_Codex" title="Dresden Codex">Dresden Codex</a> contains a <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> table which records the <a href="/wiki/Heliacal_rising" title="Heliacal rising">heliacal risings</a> of Venus. Using the GMT correlation these agree closely with modern astronomical calculations.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><b>Archaeological</b>: Various items that can be associated with specific Long Count dates have been <a href="/wiki/Isotope_dating" class="mw-redirect" title="Isotope dating">isotope dated</a>. In 1959 the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania" title="University of Pennsylvania">University of Pennsylvania</a> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dating" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon dating">carbon dated</a> samples from ten wood lintels from <a href="/wiki/Tikal" title="Tikal">Tikal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jstor_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jstor-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These were carved with a date equivalent to 741&#160;AD, using the GMT correlation. The average <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dating" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon dating">carbon date</a> was 746±34 years. Recently one of these, Lintel 3 from Temple I, was analyzed again using more accurate methods and found to agree closely with the GMT correlation.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2012,
using modern AMS radiocarbon dating, a single beam from Tikal was dated, also strongly supporting
the GMT.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>If a proposed correlation only has to agree with one of these lines of evidence there could be numerous other possibilities. Astronomers have proposed many correlations, for example: <a href="/wiki/Floyd_Lounsbury" title="Floyd Lounsbury">Lounsbury</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Fuls, <i>et al.</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-archaeoastronomie_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-archaeoastronomie-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Böhm and Böhm<sup id="cite_ref-hermetic2_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hermetic2-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-volny_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-volny-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and Stock.<sup id="cite_ref-baktun_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baktun-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Today, 31 December 2024 (<a href="/wiki/UTC" class="mw-redirect" title="UTC">UTC</a>), in the Long Count is 
13.0.12.3.13 (using GMT correlation).
</p>

<div style="clear:both;" class=""></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="2012_and_the_Long_Count">2012 and the Long Count</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerican_Long_Count_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 2012 and the Long Count"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1236090951">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2012_phenomenon" title="2012 phenomenon">2012 phenomenon</a></div>
<p>According to the <i><a href="/wiki/Popol_Vuh" title="Popol Vuh">Popol Vuh</a></i>, a book compiling details of <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_creation_accounts" class="mw-redirect" title="Mesoamerican creation accounts">creation accounts</a> known to the <a href="/wiki/K%CA%BCiche%CA%BC_people" title="Kʼicheʼ people">Kʼicheʼ Maya</a> of the Colonial-era highlands, humankind lives in the fourth world.<sup id="cite_ref-schele1_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schele1-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <i>Popol Vuh</i> describes the first three creations that the gods failed in making and the creation of the successful fourth world where men were placed. In the Maya Long Count, the previous creation ended at the end of a 13th bʼakʼtun.
</p><p>The previous creation ended on a Long Count of 12.19.19.17.19. Another 12.19.19.17.19 occurred on December 20, 2012 (Gregorian Calendar), followed by the start of the 14th bʼakʼtun, 13.0.0.0.0, on December 21, 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>f<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There are only two references to the current creation's 13th&#160;bʼakʼtun in the fragmentary Mayan corpus: <a href="/wiki/Tortuguero_(Maya_site)" title="Tortuguero (Maya site)">Tortuguero</a> Monument&#160;6, part of a ruler's inscription and the recently discovered La Corona Hieroglyphic Stairway&#160;2, Block&#160;V.<sup id="cite_ref-wordpress_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wordpress-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Maya inscriptions occasionally reference future predicted events or commemorations that would occur on dates that lie beyond 2012 (that is, beyond the completion of the 13th&#160;<i>bʼakʼtun</i> of the current era). Most of these are in the form of "distance dates" where some Long Count date is given, together with a Distance Number that is to be added to the Long Count date to arrive at this future date.
</p><p>For example, on the west panel at the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Inscriptions" class="mw-redirect" title="Temple of Inscriptions">Temple of Inscriptions</a> in <a href="/wiki/Palenque" title="Palenque">Palenque</a>, a section of the text projects into the future to the 80th&#160;Calendar Round (CR) 'anniversary' of the famous Palenque ruler <a href="/wiki/K%CA%BCinich_Janaab%CA%BC_Pakal" title="Kʼinich Janaabʼ Pakal">Kʼinich Janaabʼ Pakal</a>'s accession to the throne (Pakal's accession occurred on a Calendar Round date 5&#160;Lamat 1&#160;Mol, at Long Count 9.9.2.4.8 equivalent to 27&#160;July 615&#160;CE in the <a href="/wiki/Proleptic_Gregorian_calendar" title="Proleptic Gregorian calendar">proleptic Gregorian calendar</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>g<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It does this by commencing with Pakal's birthdate 9.8.9.13.0 &#160; 8&#160;Ajaw 13&#160;Pop (24&#160;March <span class="nowrap">603 CE Gregorian</span>) and adding to it the Distance Number 10.11.10.5.8.<sup id="cite_ref-Schele_1992,_pp.93–95_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schele_1992,_pp.93–95-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>This calculation arrives at the 80th Calendar Round since his accession, a day that also has a CR date of <span class="nowrap">5 Lamat 1 Mol</span>, but which lies over 4,000&#160;years in the future from Pakal's time—the day 21&#160;October in the year&#160;4772. The inscription notes<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> that this day would fall eight days after the completion of the 1st <i>piktun</i> (since the creation or zero date of the Long Count system), where the <i>piktun</i> is the next-highest order above the <i>bʼakʼtun</i> in the Long Count. If the completion date of that <i>piktun</i>—13&#160;October 4772—were to be written out in Long Count notation, it could be represented as 1.0.0.0.0.0. The 80th&#160;CR anniversary date, eight days later, would be 1.0.0.0.0.8 &#160; 5&#160;Lamat 1&#160;Mol.<sup id="cite_ref-Schele_1992,_pp.93–95_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schele_1992,_pp.93–95-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Despite the publicity generated by the 2012 date, Susan Milbrath, curator of Latin American Art and Archaeology at the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="Florida Museum of Natural History">Florida Museum of Natural History</a>, stated that "We have no record or knowledge that [the Maya] would think the world would come to an end" in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-Milbrath_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Milbrath-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>USA Today</i> writes <span style="padding-right:.15em;">"</span>'For the ancient Maya, it was a huge celebration to make it to the end of a whole cycle,' says Sandra Noble, executive director of the Foundation for the Advancement of Mesoamerican Studies in <a href="/wiki/Crystal_River,_Florida" title="Crystal River, Florida">Crystal River, Florida</a>. To render December 21, 2012, as a <a href="/wiki/Doomsday_event" class="mw-redirect" title="Doomsday event">doomsday event</a> or moment of cosmic shifting, she says, is 'a complete fabrication and a chance for a lot of people to cash in.<span style="padding-right:.15em;">'</span>"<sup id="cite_ref-Milbrath_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Milbrath-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> "There will be another cycle," says E. Wyllys Andrews V, director of the <a href="/wiki/Tulane_University" title="Tulane University">Tulane University</a> Middle American Research Institute (MARI). "We know the Maya thought there was one before this, and that implies they were comfortable with the idea of another one after this."<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Converting_between_the_Long_Count_and_western_calendars">Converting between the Long Count and western calendars</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerican_Long_Count_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Converting between the Long Count and western calendars"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Calculating_a_Western_calendar_date_from_a_Long_Count">Calculating a Western calendar date from a Long Count</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerican_Long_Count_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Calculating a Western calendar date from a Long Count"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>It is important to know the difference between the <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">Gregorian</a> calendars when calculating a Western calendar date from a Long Count date.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>h<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Using as an example the Long Count date of 9.10.11.17.0 (Long Count date mentioned on the Palenque Palace Tablet), first calculate the number of days that have passed since the zero date (August 11, 3114 BCE; GMT correlation, in the <a href="/wiki/Proleptic_Gregorian_calendar" title="Proleptic Gregorian calendar">Proleptic Gregorian calendar</a>, September 6, &#8722;3113 <a href="/wiki/Proleptic_Julian_calendar" title="Proleptic Julian calendar">Julian</a> astronomical).
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="margin:1em auto;">

<tbody><tr align="right">
<td>9</td>
<td>× 144,000</td>
<td>= 1,296,000
</td></tr>
<tr align="right">
<td>10</td>
<td>× 7,200</td>
<td>= 72,000
</td></tr>
<tr align="right">
<td>11</td>
<td>× 360</td>
<td>= 3,960
</td></tr>
<tr align="right">
<td>17</td>
<td>× 20</td>
<td>= 340
</td></tr>
<tr align="right">
<td>0</td>
<td>× 1</td>
<td>= 0
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td></td>
<td>Total days</td>
<td>= 1,372,300
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Then add the GMT correlation to the total number of days.
</p>
<dl><dd>1,372,300 + 584,283 = 1,956,583</dd></dl>
<p>This number is a <b><a href="/wiki/Julian_day" title="Julian day">Julian day</a></b>.
</p><p>To convert a <a href="/wiki/Julian_day" title="Julian day">Julian day</a> to a <a href="/wiki/Proleptic_Gregorian_calendar" title="Proleptic Gregorian calendar">Proleptic Gregorian calendar</a> date:<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>From this number, subtract the nearest smaller Julian Day Number (in the table below), in this case 1,940,206, which corresponds to the year 600&#160;CE.
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<tbody><tr>
<th>year</th>
<th>JDN:</th>

<th>JDN:
</th></tr>

<td>1,721,060</td>
<td>1100</td>
<td>2,122,827
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>100</td>
<td>1,757,585</td>

</td></tr>

</td></tr>

<td>1400</td>

<td>1500</td>

</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>500</td>
<td>1,903,682</td>
<td>1600</td>
<td>2,305,448
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>600</td>
<td>1,940,206</td>
<td>1700</td>
<td>2,341,973
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>700</td>
<td>1,976,730</td>
<td>1800</td>
<td>2,378,497
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>800</td>
<td>2,013,254</td>
<td>1900</td>
<td>2,415,021
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>900</td>
<td>2,049,779</td>
<td>2000</td>
<td>2,451,545
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>1000</td>
<td>2,086,303
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<dl><dd>1,956,583 – 1,940,206 = 16,377</dd></dl>
<p>Next, divide this number by 365&#160;days (vague year).
</p>
<dl><dd>16,377 / 365 = 44.86849</dd></dl>
<p>The remainder is 44.86849&#160;years, which is 44&#160;years and 317&#160;days. The full year date is 644&#160;CE. Now calculate the month and day number, taking into account leap days over the 44&#160;years. In the Gregorian Calendar, every fourth year is a leap year with the exception of centuries not evenly divisible by 400 (e.g. 100, 200, 300). When the year is divisible by 400 (e.g. 400, 800, etc.), do not add an extra day. The calculated year is 644&#160;CE. The number of leap days, keeping in mind that the year 600 is not a leap year, is 10. Subtracting that from 317 remainder days is 307; in other words, the 307th day of the year 644&#160;CE, which is November&#160;3.
To summarize: the Long Count date 9.10.11.17.0 corresponds to November&#160;3, 644&#160;CE, in the <a href="/wiki/Proleptic_Gregorian_calendar" title="Proleptic Gregorian calendar">Proleptic Gregorian calendar</a>.
</p><p>To convert a <a href="/wiki/Julian_day" title="Julian day">Julian day</a> to a Julian/Gregorian astronomical date (<a href="/wiki/Proleptic_Julian_calendar" title="Proleptic Julian calendar">Proleptic Julian calendar</a> before 46&#160;BCE):
</p><p>Use an astronomical algorithm such as the Method of <a href="/wiki/Jean_Meeus" title="Jean Meeus">Meeus</a><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> to convert the <a href="/wiki/Julian_day" title="Julian day">Julian day</a> to a Julian/Gregorian date with astronomical dating of negative years:<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>i<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In this example:
</p>
<pre><b>input:</b> Julian day <i>J</i>
<i>J</i> = <i>J</i> + 0.5    <i>// 1,956,583.5</i>
<i>Z</i> = integer part of <i>J</i>           <i>// 1,956,583</i>
<i>F</i> = fraction part of <i>J</i>          <i>// 0.5</i>

<b>if</b> <i>Z</i> &lt; 2,299,161 <b>then</b> <i>// Julian?</i>
    <i>A</i> = <i>Z</i>
<b>else</b>
    <i>alpha</i> = floor((<i>Z</i> - 1,867,216.25) / 36,524.25)   <i>// 15</i>
    <i>A</i> = <i>Z</i> + 1 + <i>alpha</i> - floor(<i>alpha</i> / 4.0)          <i>// 2,436,129</i>
    <i>// The floor operation rounds a decimal number down to the next lowest integer.</i>
    <i>// For example, floor(1.5) = 1 and floor(−1.5) = -2</i>
<b>end if</b>

<i>B</i> = <i>A</i> + 1524                     <i>// 1,958,107</i>
<i>C</i> = floor((<i>B</i> - 122.1) / 365.25)  <i>// 5,360</i>
<i>D</i> = floor(365.25 × <i>C</i>)            <i>// 1,957,740</i>
<i>E</i> = floor((<i>B</i> - <i>D</i>) / 30.6001)     <i>// 11</i>
<i>day</i> = <i>B</i> - <i>D</i> - floor(30.6001 × <i>E</i>) + <i>F</i>   <i>// 31.5</i>

<b>if</b> <i>E</i> &lt; 14 <b>then</b>
    <i>month</i> = <i>E</i> - 1   <i>// 10</i>
<b>else</b>
    <i>month</i> = <i>E</i> - 13
<b>end if</b>

<b>if</b> <i>month</i> &gt; 2 <b>then</b>
    <i>year</i> = <i>C</i> - 4716    <i>// 644</i>
<b>else</b>
    <i>year</i> = <i>C</i> - 4715
<b>end if</b>

<b>return</b> (<i>year</i>, <i>month</i>, <i>day</i>)
</pre>
<p>In this example the <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian</a> date is noon October 31, 644. The Method of <a href="/wiki/Jean_Meeus" title="Jean Meeus">Meeus</a> is not valid for negative year numbers (astronomical), so another method such as the method of Peter Baum<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> should be used.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Calculating_a_full_Long_Count_date">Calculating a full Long Count date</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerican_Long_Count_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Calculating a full Long Count date"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Morley_1915_ISglyphs.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Morley_1915_ISglyphs.svg/160px-Morley_1915_ISglyphs.svg.png" decoding="async" width="160" height="518" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Morley_1915_ISglyphs.svg/240px-Morley_1915_ISglyphs.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Morley_1915_ISglyphs.svg/320px-Morley_1915_ISglyphs.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="272" data-file-height="880" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Chichen_Itza" title="Chichen Itza">Chichen Itza</a> Initial Series inscription. This date (glyphs A2, B2, ..., A5) is 10.2.9.1.9 &#160; 9&#160;Muluk 7&#160;Sak, equivalent to July 28, 878 (GMT Gregorian).</figcaption></figure>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1251242444"><table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Mesoamerican_Long_Count_calendar" title="Special:EditPage/Mesoamerican Long Count calendar">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>&#32;in this section. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">August 2020</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>A full Long Count date not only includes the five digits of the Long Count, but the 2&#160;character Tzolkʼin and the two-character Haabʼ dates as well. The five digit Long Count can therefore be confirmed with the other four characters (the "calendar round date").
</p><p>Taking as an example a Calendar Round date of 9.12.2.0.16 (Long Count) 5&#160;Kibʼ (Tzolkʼin) 14&#160;Yaxkʼin (Haabʼ). One can check whether this date is correct by the following calculation.
</p><p>It is perhaps easier to find out how many days there are since 4&#160;Ajaw 8&#160;Kumkʼu and show how the date 5&#160;Kibʼ 14&#160;Yaxkʼin is derived.
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="margin:1em auto;">

<tbody><tr align="right">
<td>9</td>
<td>× 144000</td>
<td>= 1296000
</td></tr>
<tr align="right">
<td>12</td>
<td>× 7200</td>
<td>= 86400
</td></tr>
<tr align="right">
<td>2</td>
<td>× 360</td>
<td>= 720
</td></tr>
<tr align="right">
<td>0</td>
<td>× 20</td>
<td>= 0
</td></tr>
<tr align="right">
<td>16</td>
<td>× 1</td>
<td>= 16
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td></td>
<td>Total days</td>
<td>= 1383136
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Calculating_the_Tzolkʼin_date_portion"><span id="Calculating_the_Tzolk.CA.BCin_date_portion"></span>Calculating the Tzolkʼin date portion</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerican_Long_Count_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Calculating the Tzolkʼin date portion"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The Tzolkʼin date is counted forward from 4&#160;Ajaw. To calculate the numerical portion of the Tzolkʼin date, add 4 to the total number of days given by the date and then divide total number of days by 13.
</p>
<dl><dd>(4 + 1,383,136) / 13 = 106,395 (and 5/13)</dd></dl>
<p>This means that 106,395 whole 13&#160;day cycles have been completed and the numerical portion of the Tzolkʼin date is 5.
</p><p>To calculate the day, divide the total number of days in the long count by 20 since there are twenty day names.
</p>
<dl><dd>1,383,136 / 20 = 69,156 (and 16/20)</dd></dl>
<p>This means 16&#160;day names must be counted from Ajaw. This gives Kibʼ. Therefore, the Tzolkʼin date is 5&#160;Kibʼ.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Calculating_the_Haabʼ_date_portion"><span id="Calculating_the_Haab.CA.BC_date_portion"></span>Calculating the Haabʼ date portion</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerican_Long_Count_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Calculating the Haabʼ date portion"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The Haabʼ date 8&#160;Kumkʼu is the ninth day of the eighteenth month. There are 17&#160;days to the start of the next year.
</p><p>Subtract 17&#160;days from the total, to find how many complete Haabʼ years are contained.
</p>
<dl><dd>1,383,136 − 17 = 1,383,119</dd></dl>
<pre>by 365
</pre>
<dl><dd>1,383,119 / 365 = 3,789 and (134/365)</dd></dl>
<p>Therefore, 3,789&#160;complete Haabʼ have passed and the remainder 134 is the 135th&#160;day in the new Haabʼ, since a remainder of 0 would indicate the first day.
</p><p>Find which month the day is in. Dividing the remainder 134 by 20, is six complete months and a remainder of 14, indicating the 15th&#160;day. So, the date in the Haabʼ lies in the seventh month, which is Yaxkʼin. The fifteenth day of Yaxkʼin is 14, thus the Haabʼ date is 14&#160;Yaxkʼin.
</p><p>So the date of the long count date 9.12.2.0.16 &#160; 5&#160;Kibʼ 14&#160;Yaxkʼin is confirmed.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Piktuns_and_higher_orders">Piktuns and higher orders</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerican_Long_Count_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Piktuns and higher orders"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>There are also four rarely used higher-order periods above the bʼakʼtun: <i>piktun</i>, <i>kalabtun</i>, <i>kʼinchiltun</i> and <i>alautun</i>. All of these words are inventions of Mayanists. Each one consists of 20 of the lesser units.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>j<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Many inscriptions give the date of the current creation as a large number of 13s preceding 13.0.0.0.0 &#160; 4&#160;Ahau 8&#160;Kumkʼu. For example, a Late Classic monument from <a href="/wiki/Coba" title="Coba">Coba</a>, Stela&#160;1. The date of creation is expressed as 13.13.13.13.13.13.13.13.13.13.13.13.13.13.13.13.13.13.13.13.0.0.0.0, where the units are 13s in the nineteen places larger than the bʼakʼtun.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-famsi2_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-famsi2-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some authors think that the 13s were symbolic of a completion and did not represent an actual number.<sup id="cite_ref-Lloyd_B._Anderson_2008_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lloyd_B._Anderson_2008-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Most inscriptions that use these are in the form of distance dates and Long Reckonings – they give a base date, a distance number that is added or subtracted and the resulting Long Count.
</p><p>The first example below is from Schele (1987). The second is from Stuart (2005 pp.&#160;60, 77)<sup id="cite_ref-distances_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-distances-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Palenque Temple of the Cross, tablet, Schele (1987 p.)<br />
12.19.13.4.0 &#160; 8&#160;Ajaw 18&#160;Tzek in the prior era<br />
6.14.0 Distance number linking to the "era date"<br />
13.0.0.0.0 &#160; 4&#160;Ajaw 8&#160;Kumkʼu
</p><p>Palenque Temple&#160;XIX, South Panel G2-H6 Stuart (2005 pp.&#160;60, 77)<br />
12.10.1.13.2 &#160; 9&#160;Ikʼ 5&#160;Mol &#160; (seating of GI in the prior era)<br />
2.8.3.8.0<br />
1.18.5.3.2 &#160; 9&#160;Ikʼ 15&#160;Keh &#160; (rebirth of GI, this date also in Temple of the Cross)
</p><p>The tablet of the inscriptions contains this inscription:<sup id="cite_ref-Lloyd_B._Anderson_2008_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lloyd_B._Anderson_2008-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><br />
9.8.9.13.0 &#160; 8&#160;Ajaw 13&#160;Pop<br />
10.11.10.5.8<br />
1.0.0.0.0.8
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Dresden_codex" class="mw-redirect" title="Dresden codex">Dresden codex</a> contains another method for writing distance numbers. These are Ring Numbers. Specific dates within the 
Dresden codex are often given by calculations involving Ring Numbers. <a href="/wiki/Ernst_F%C3%B6rstemann" title="Ernst Förstemann">Förstemann</a><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> identified these, but Wilson (1924)<sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Pages: 24–25">&#58;&#8202;24–25&#8202;</span></sup> later clarified the way in which they operate. Ring Numbers are intervals of days between the Era Base date 4&#160;Ajaw 8&#160;Kumkʼu and an earlier Ring Base date, where the place-holder for the numeral of days in the interval is circled by an image of a tied red band. Added to this earlier Ring Base date is another count of days forward, which <a href="/wiki/J._Eric_S._Thompson" title="J. Eric S. Thompson">Thompson</a><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> refers to as a Long Round, leading to a final date within the Long Count that is given as an entry date to be used within a specific table in the codex.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Ring number&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;(12) 12.12.17.3.1 &#160; 13&#160;Imix 9&#160;Wo (7.2.14.19 before (13) 13.0.0.0.0)<br />
distance number (0) 10.13.13.3.2<br />
Long Count&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;&#160;10.6.10.6.3 &#160; 13&#160;Akʼbal 1&#160;Kankʼin
</p><p>Ring number (portion of the DN preceding era date)  7.2.14.19<br />
Add Ring number to the ring number date to reach 13.0.0.0.0
</p><p><a href="/wiki/J._Eric_S._Thompson" title="J. Eric S. Thompson">Thompson</a><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> contains a table of typical long reckonings after Satterwaite.<sup id="cite_ref-distances_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-distances-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The "Serpent Numbers" in the Dresden codex pp.&#160;61–69 is a table of dates using a base date of 1.18.1.8.0.16 in the prior era (5,482,096 days).<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Notes">Notes</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mesoamerican_Long_Count_calendar&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Notes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1239543626">.mw-parser-output .reflist{margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}@media screen{.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%}}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-columns references-column-width reflist-lower-alpha">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The correlation between the Long Count and Western calendars is calculated according to the one used by a majority of Maya researchers, known as the (modified) GMT or Goodman-Martinez-Thompson correlation. An alternate correlation sometimes used puts the starting date two days later. August 11, 3114&#160;BCE is a date in the <a href="/wiki/Proleptic_Gregorian_calendar" title="Proleptic Gregorian calendar">proleptic Gregorian calendar</a>, which is to September 6, &#8722;3113 <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian</a> <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_year_numbering" title="Astronomical year numbering">astronomical</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Notable in this sequence is the glyph with nine variant forms labeled G by early epigraphers. It has been connected with the cycle of Lords of the Night known from colonial era sources in Central Mexico but alternate explanations have also been offered. See Thompson.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">To clarify, there are Long Count inscriptions which refer to dates earlier than the 1st century BCE, but these were carved much later in a retrospective fashion.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">All extant Maya inscriptions that represent this base date wrote it with a "13" bakʼtuns, not "0". But when using "13.0.0.0.0" as a base date in calculations, the "13" bakʼtuns has the numerical value 0, as if it were written as "<i>0</i>.0.0.0.0". This is easily confused when the "13" bakʼtuns has the actual value 13 in the <i>current</i> baktʼun, as in the Maya date for today: 
13.0.12.3.13 (=20:07, Tuesday, December 31, 2024 (<a href="/wiki/UTC" class="mw-redirect" title="UTC">UTC</a>)).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-40">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to Thompson<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> "The point from which the moon age is counted is not surely known. The possibilities are disappearance of the old moon, conjunction, or appearance of the new moon ... Beyer (1973a) believed that the calculation was made from the disappearance of the old moon. The latter method of counting (disappearance of the old moon) is still current in some Tzeltal, Chol, and Tzotzil villages in Chiapas&#160;..."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Various sources place this on other dates, notably on December 23.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-64"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-64">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Gregorian, using GMT correlation JDN=584283.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-69"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-69">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 46 BC Julius Caesar adopted the convention of having three years of twelve months of approximately 30&#160;days each to make a year of 365&#160;days and a leap year of 366&#160;days. This made the length of the civil year 365.25&#160;days, close to the length of the solar year of 365.2422&#160;days. This is the <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian calendar</a>. By 1582 there was an appreciable discrepancy between the <a href="/wiki/Winter_solstice" title="Winter solstice">winter solstice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">Christmas</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Equinox" title="Equinox">spring equinox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">Easter</a>. <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_XIII" title="Pope Gregory XIII">Pope Gregory XIII</a>, with the help of Italian astronomer <a href="/wiki/Aloysius_Lilius" title="Aloysius Lilius">Aloysius Lilius</a> (<a href="/wiki/Luigi_Lilio" class="mw-redirect" title="Luigi Lilio">Luigi Lilio</a>), reformed this system by abolishing the days October 5 through October 14, 1582. This brought the civil and tropical years closer together. He also missed three days every four centuries by decreeing that centuries are only leap years if they are evenly divisible by 400. So for example 1700, 1800 and 1900 are not leap years but 1600 and 2000 are. This is the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">Gregorian calendar</a>.

Astronomical calculations will return a year zero and years before that are negative numbers. This is astronomical dating. Years before 46&#160;BC are calculated as <a href="/wiki/Proleptic_Julian_calendar" title="Proleptic Julian calendar">proleptic Julian dates</a>. There is no year zero in historical dating. In historical dating the year 1&#160;BCE is followed by the year 1&#160;CE
For example, the year &#8722;3113 (astronomical dating) is the same as 3114&#160;BCE (historical dating).

Many books about the Maya and many computer programs to do Maya calendar conversions use the <a href="/wiki/Proleptic_Gregorian_calendar" title="Proleptic Gregorian calendar">Proleptic Gregorian calendar</a>. In this calendar all dates before the start of the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">Gregorian calendar</a> are revised as if the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">Gregorian calendar</a> had been in use before its adoption in October&#160;1582. Although this system is popular among <a href="/wiki/Mayanists" class="mw-redirect" title="Mayanists">Mayanists</a>, it is rarely used by anyone else, so for example, dates converted using this system are useless for the study of <a href="/wiki/Maya_Astronomy" class="mw-redirect" title="Maya Astronomy">Maya Astronomy</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-72"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-72">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Astronomical algorithms calculate a day as a decimal number equal to a day and a fraction of a day. The Julian date starts at noon. <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_year_numbering" title="Astronomical year numbering">Astronomical dating</a> has a year&#160;0. In historical dating the year 1&#160;BCE is followed by the year 1&#160;CE. Astronomical years before 0 are written with a negative sign. For example, the year 3114&#160;BCE is the year &#8722;3113 astronomical.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-76"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-76">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"...&#160;we have clear evidence that the current Bakʼtun cycle does not conclude at 13, as the last one did, but advances to 20. In other words, 13.0.0.0.0 will be followed by 14.0.0.0.0, 15.0.0.0.0, and so on to 19.0.0.0.0&#160;. A text at the site of Palenque, Mexico, makes this very plain when it records the completion of 1&#160;Piktun, the next unit above the Bakʼtun, in 4772&#160;CE.</span>
</li>
</ol></div>

</li>
<li id="cite_note-ochoa1983-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-ochoa1983_13-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ochoa1983_13-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ochoa1983_13-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222"><cite id="CITEREFOchoaLee1983" class="citation book cs1 cs1-prop-foreign-lang-source">Ochoa, Lorenzo; Lee, Thomas A., eds. <li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTERecinos195333_Recinos_gives_2&amp;nbsp;Tihax_(Etzʼnabʼ)_is_September_10,_1541(&#91;&#91;Julian_calendar&#124;Julian&#93;&#93;)._The_date_which_marked_the_destruction_of_the_city_of_Guatemala_founded_at_the_foot_of_the_Volcano_of_Agua._&quot;After_a_period_of_heavy_rain_there_was_an_earthquake_and_then_a_flood_of_water_(a_&#91;&#91;lahar&#93;&#93;)_that_destroyed_the_old_city_of_Guatemala&quot;-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTERecinos195333_Recinos_gives_2&amp;nbsp;Tihax_(Etzʼnabʼ)_is_September_10,_1541([[Julian_calendar|Julian]])._The_date_which_marked_the_destruction_of_the_city_of_Guatemala_founded_at_the_foot_of_the_Volcano_of_Agua._&quot;After_a_period_of_heavy_rain_there_was_an_earthquake_and_then_a_flood_of_water_(a_[[lahar]])_that_destroyed_the_old_city_of_Guatemala&quot;_29-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFRecinos1953">Recinos 1953</a>, p.&#160;33 Recinos gives 2&#160;Tihax (Etzʼnabʼ) is September 10, 1541(<a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian</a>). The date which marked the destruction of the city of Guatemala founded at the foot of the Volcano of Agua. "After a period of heavy rain there was an earthquake and then a flood of water (a <a href="/wiki/Lahar" title="Lahar">lahar</a>) that destroyed the old city of Guatemala".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-30">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222"><cite id="CITEREFWeeksSachsePrager2013" class="citation book cs1">Weeks, John M.; Sachse, Frauke; Prager, Christian M. (15 May 2013). <i>Maya Daykeeping: Three Calendars from Highland Guatemala (Mesoamerican Worlds)</i>. University Press of Colorado. pp.&#160;<span class="nowrap">176–</span>84. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1607322467" title="Special:BookSources/978-1607322467"><bdi>978-1607322467</bdi></a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Maya+Daykeeping%3A+Three+Calendars+from+Highland+Guatemala+%28Mesoamerican+Worlds%29&amp;rft.pages=%3Cspan+class%3D%22nowrap%22%3E176-%3C%2Fspan%3E84&amp;rft.pub=University+Press+of+Colorado&amp;rft.date=2013-05-15&amp;rft.isbn=978-1607322467&amp;rft.aulast=Weeks&amp;rft.aufirst=John+M.&amp;rft.au=Sachse%2C+Frauke&amp;rft.au=Prager%2C+Christian+M.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMesoamerican+Long+Count+calendar" class="Z3988"></span> In Appendix&#160;2, <i>Notes on the Correlation of Maya and Gregorian Calendars</i>, the authors give the examples of December 9, 1722 = 8&#160;Kej 20&#160;Ukabʼ Siʼj (8&#160;Manikʼ 0&#160;Yax) and December 9, 1723 = 9 E 20 Ukabʼ Siʼj (9 Ebʼ 0 Yax). "Using Thompson's correlation constant of A = 584,283, all values from the calendar round correspond with the information in the 1722&#160;Kʼicheʼ calendar."</span>

<li id="cite_note-74"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-74">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFThompson1960">Thompson 1960</a>, pp.&#160;314, 316, 148–49<span class="error harv-error" style="display: none; font-size:100%"> harvnb error: no target: CITEREFThompson1960 (<a href="/wiki/Category:Harv_and_Sfn_template_errors" title="Category:Harv and Sfn template errors">help</a>)</span> Appendix IV: "I have throughout assumed that the baktuns were grouped, not in 13's, but in 20's, for the evidence supporting a vigesimal count of baktuns in Dresden and at Palenque and Copan is too strong to be overridden."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-75"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-75">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222"><cite id="CITEREFGrofe2007" class="citation book cs1">Grofe, Michael John (2007). <i>The Serpent Series: Precession in the Maya Dresden Codex</i>. p.&#160;55. <q>On occasion, the Maya also recorded intervals of time even greater than 13&#160;Bʼakʼtuns, such as one Piktun, composed of 20&#160;Bʼakʼtuns. This is relevant to the current discussion concerning the Serpent Series.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Serpent+Series%3A+Precession+in+the+Maya+Dresden+Codex&amp;rft.pages=55&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft.aulast=Grofe&amp;rft.aufirst=Michael+John&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMesoamerican+Long+Count+calendar" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
